3.11 Fungi and Lichens (non-vascular flora)
3.11.1 Introduction
This document corresponds to the baseline report of fungi and lichens, whose characterization
on the ground it was carried out during the months of August 2022 and July d
the year 2023.
The characterization of these components in the area of influence of the Project allows defining the line of
basis in accordance with the provisions of Law No. 19,300 on General Bases of the Environment and
article 18 of the Regulation of the Environmental Impact Assessment System (RSEIA), S.D. No. 40 of 2012
modified by D.S. N°30/2023, both from the MMA; This will subsequently allow the identification and
evaluation of potential environmental effects generated by the execution of the Project on these
components.
For these components, the objectives, methodology used and the results are presented in a form
independent, due to its different nature, placing emphasis on the presence of species of
national importance, species with limited distribution, species of scientific interest or species
classified in some conservation category.
3.11.2 Objectives
3.11.2.1 General Objective
Characterize the fungi (macromycetes) and lichen components of the Project influence area (AI),
in accordance with the provisions of article 18 e.2 of the Regulations of the Impact Assessment System
Environment (RSEIA) for Terrestrial Ecosystems (MMA, 2012).
3.11.2.2 Specific objectives
To meet the proposed general objective, the following specific objectives have been defined:
• Identify the species of fungi (macromycetes) and lichens in the area of influence of the Project.
• Establish the location and distribution of fungal species (macromycetes) and lichens
identified in the area of influence of the Project.
• Determine the diversity of species of fungi (macromycetes) and lichens identified in the area
of influence of the Project, which will be addressed through the parameter of species richness.
• Establish the abundance of species of fungi (macromycetes) and lichens recorded in the area
of influence of the Project, which will be addressed through the frequency of the species.
• Generate a catalog of the species of fungi (macromycetes) and lichens recorded in the area
of influence of the Project, indicating scientific name and taxonomic classification.
3-156
==================================================================================
PAGE 167
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
• Identify the species of fungi (macromycetes) and lichens recorded for the area of
influence of the Project that are classified in any category of conservation of
in accordance with the provisions of article 37 of Law No. 19,300.
3.11.3 Area of influence
The area of influence (AI) for the fungal and lichen components (Figure 3-76) corresponds to the extension
space in which the environmental impacts of the Project can be manifested, and is established with the
purpose of defining whether the project or activity generates or presents any of the effects, characteristics or
circumstances of article No. 11 of Law No. 19,300, or to justify the nonexistence of said effects,
characteristics or circumstances”, as indicated in article N°2, letter a) of D.S. N°40/2012, as well as
the Guide on the Area of Influence in the SEIA, Exempt Resolution No. 0423 of the Executive Directorate of the SEA,
dated April 26, 2017.
The AI corresponds to all those surfaces where the parts, works and actions associated with the Project,
both permanent and temporary, will exercise actions over these organizations, mainly
those works at ground level that generate loss of specimens. This is because the construction of
These works have the effect of total or partial removal of vegetation and, therefore, the loss of fungi.
and lichens associated with these areas. Additionally, neighboring sectors are considered that do not present
works that could generate impacts, considering the natural limits of the plant formations that
They can cause the presence of habitats for the group of fungi and lichens. According to this the AI of
Fungi and Lichens reaches 673.62 hectares (Figure 3-76). Annex 3B-28 presents the AI of the
component in digital format.

3.11.4 Methodology
3.11.4.1 Literature review
A review of bibliographic background was carried out, which aimed to contextualize
environmentally the area of influence of the Project, in addition to establishing an adequate understanding of
the groups characterized in the present study (fungi and lichens), as well as the compilation of
background of these organizations, both in the Project AI, and at a regional and environmental scale.
3.11.4.2 Characterization of fungi and lichens in the AI
For an adequate characterization of the fungal (macromycete) and lichen components present in the
AI of the Project, first of all, a land survey was carried out, in which it sought to represent
all the environments present in the AI with feasibility for the development of the characterized organisms.
This characterization included several stages: sampling design, collection of terrain information,
laboratory work with the collections (samples) obtained in the field and finally analysis of the
data obtained. Each of these stages is detailed below.
Yo. Sampling design
The sampling design considered the following three (3) criteria: type of sampling, sampling unit and
sample size.
The type of sampling was targeted stratified, with the stratum being defined by environment. These environments
were defined on the basis of available satellite images, mainly those of the
Google Earth© platform.
The sampling unit was the sampling point, which sought to represent the defined environments.
in the previous analysis and in which all the species present were recorded, thus generating,
the database of fungi (macromycetes) and lichens present in the area of influence of the Project.
The sample size for the characterization of these components corresponds to the number of points
defined sampling areas, which present a uniform distribution throughout the area of influence, which
seeks to represent all present environments, with feasibility for the development of these organisms.
From this analysis, 31 sampling points were defined (Figure 3-77). Table 3-68 indicates their
location coordinates (Datum UTM WGS 84, zone 18 South) and altitude. Based on land use
present in the project's vegetation map and what was observed on the ground, the environment in which
were inserted. Finally, in Annex 3B-28 you can find the points in kmz format
and shp.

ii. Information gathering
Two campaigns were carried out to collect terrain information in the area of influence of the
Project, the first between August 1 and 7, 2022, corresponding to the winter season
3-161
==================================================================================
PAGE 172
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
2022, and the second between July 25 and 29, 2023, corresponding to the winter season
2023. In these campaigns, the sampling points were accessed by vehicle, to later
be traveled on foot. A search for fruiting was carried out at the sampling points.
or colonies of the organisms studied, mainly in sectors that present conditions
conducive to the appearance of these organisms, such as the presence of humidity, presence of species
vegetables, to which fungal species may be associated; presence of organic matter (e.g.
dead wood, accumulation of leaf litter, animal manure, etc.) and presence of elements such as rocks and
trunks that serve as substrate for lichens. This type of sampling, also called sampling
opportunistic, is the one traditionally used and the one that best suits this type of studies due to its
efficiency to characterize large surfaces, since it maximizes the probabilities of detecting the
presence of these organisms, allowing us to better approximate the total species richness of the
studied area (Lodge et al., 2004; Schmit & Lodge, 2005; Stephenson et al., 2013).
At the time of locating any fruiting or colony of the organisms studied, processes were carried out
standards for the collection and treatment of these, such as those described by Lodge et al. (2004),
Rossman et al. (1998) and Will-Wolf et al. (2004), which in general terms include in situ registration
of morphological characteristics of taxonomic importance such as habit, shape, color, presence and type of
structures, in addition to the substrate in which it develops, among others. Subsequently, the
fruiting or colony from its substrate, avoiding damaging its structures, generally with the help of
a knife or scissors. In the case of lichens and some non-lichenized fungi, the collection is maintained
with a portion of the substrate, either to avoid disintegration of the sample or due to the impossibility
to separate the organism from the substrate. In specific cases it was necessary to photograph the structure as
to record some macroscopic characteristics that are lost with subsequent treatments.
Finally, all the collections obtained in the field were dehydrated and deposited in bags of
paper, labeled with the collection data, for conservation and subsequent observation in the laboratory.
iii. Laboratory methodology
For curing and subsequent analysis and identification, the mushroom collections were transferred to the
Fungilab laboratory, where its analysis was carried out by the mycologist responsible for this study, in
so much so that the lichen collections were transferred to the Federico Johow Herbarium of the University
Metropolitan Education Sciences (UMCE), where they were in charge of a lichenologist
participant. The laboratory methodologies used for the identification of the organisms under study
considered morphological observations of taxonomic importance, both macroscopic, through
magnifying glass
stereoscopic, as well as microscopic, through an optical microscope, mounting rehydrated sections
of collections on some means of assembly.
These montages mainly considered cross sections of the fruiting or thallus and/or of
the structures of sexual reproduction, in addition to observing spores if they are present
(Figure 3-78). The mounting media used for microscopic observations were water,
3-162
==================================================================================
PAGE 173
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Potassium hydroxide (KOH), from 3 to 25%, some iodinated substance such as Lugol or Melzer's reagent, Red
Congo and cotton blue with lactophenol. Additionally, and specifically to recognize some characters
chemicals in lichenized fungi, K (10% and 25% saturated KOH solution), C (10% and 25%
sodium hypochlorite aqueous solution at 5%), KC (application of K followed by C), P (alcoholic solution of
paraphenylenediamine) and an aqueous solution of 60% concentrated nitric acid. These processes are
were carried out according to the methodologies proposed by Baral (1992), Largent et al. (1977), Orange et al. (2001)
and Wright & Albertó (2002, 2006).
Once these processes were completed, the collections were curated for entry into the
personal collections of the specialists participating in this study. Later, they were
entered into the collection of the National Museum of Natural History (SGO) in the case of mushrooms and
Federico Johow herbarium (UMCE), for lichens.
Figure 3-78. Example of measurement of spore parameters.
Source: GAC
iv. Data analysis
The organisms registered for the area of influence were characterized according to the following attributes:
a) Identity and taxonomic position
The taxonomic data obtained, both in the field and in the laboratory, were analyzed using
specific literature for each biological group studied, which is also variable according to the
species that are recorded in the field, proceeding to determine the specimens at the species level
or at a higher level, depending on whether the appropriate taxonomic characters are available for each level.
taxonomic. Furthermore, due to the frequent phenotypic plasticity of these organisms, in some
Sometimes comparison with herbarium materials was necessary for correct identification.
3-163
==================================================================================
PAGE 174
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
The recorded species were characterized according to the taxonomic categories Kingdom, Phylum, Class,
Order, Family, Gender and Species. For fungi, the criteria of the tenth edition of the Dictionary were followed
of the Fungi (Kirk et al. 2008) and/or MycoBank (www.mycobank.org), and as a nomenclatural reference
The Index Fungorum (IF, www.indexfungorum.org) was followed. For the classification of lichens, the
scheme of Lumbsch & Huhndorf (2010) and specifically for the species belonging to the Phylum
Ascomycota, the criterion of Hibbett et al. (2007), Jacklitsch et al. (2016) and the recommendations of the
AFTOL (Assembling the Fungal Tree of Life) project available online at www.aftol.umn.edu. This
project proposes the search and elaboration of the tree of life of the organisms belonging to the Kingdom
Fungi, based on phylogenetic analyzes of a wide range of fungal organisms, which has resulted in
a series of systematic modifications and new taxonomic proposals (James et al., 2006; Schoch et
al., 2009).
b) Spatial and environmental distribution of the recorded species
The results of findings of the organisms studied were grouped according to the points of
sampling in which these were carried out, in addition, the type of environment (land use) in which
that point was inserted.
c) Absolute frequency and relative frequency of the recorded species
The parameters absolute frequency and relative frequency were used, with the objective of
or to obtain a value
approximate abundance of each of the recorded species. In simple terms, the frequency
absolute indicates the percentage of points, with respect to the total sampling, in which the species is found.
The absolute frequency was calculated through the following formula:
𝑃𝑀𝑖
𝐹 (%)= ×100
𝑖 𝑃𝑀𝑇

d) Conservation categories
In accordance with the provisions of Memorandum DJ N°387/2008 (CONAMA, 2008), which establishes the
order of priority for the purposes of environmental evaluation of the classifications and/or categorizations of the
species of wild flora and fauna. To establish the conservation category of the species present
In the area of influence, the documents that apply to the components studied are the processes
species classification officials according to D.S. N°29/2011 (MMA, 2011) which establishes the current
Regulations for the Classification of Wild Species (hereinafter RCE), that is, the D.S. N°38/2015
(MMA, 2015), D.S. N°6/2017 (MMA, 2017), D.S. N°79/2018 (MMA, 2018), D.S. N°23/2019 (MMA, 2020a),

(MMA, 2024), corresponding to the approval and officialization of the eleventh, thirteenth, tenth
fourth, fifteenth, sixteenth, seventeenth, eighteenth and nineteenth process of
classification of species according to conservation categories.
It should be mentioned that the proposed classification of species of
lichens by Quilhot et al. (1998), because, starting from the seventeenth classification process of
species according to conservation categories, the 59 species included in this proposal are already
are re-evaluated using the criteria of the International Union for Conservation of Nature (IUCN),
currently in force.
3.11.5 Results
3.11.5.1 Literature review
i. General background of the mycobiota: Fungi and lichens
The mycobiota can be defined generally as the set of fungal organisms or fungi.
present in a given space and in a given period of time (Kirk et al., 2008).
From the current systematic point of view, the groups traditionally known as fungi are not
would be found as closely related as previously thought and would belong to at least three kingdoms
distinct: the kingdoms Fungi, Stramenopila and four phyla belonging to the kingdom Protozoa (Alexopoulos et
al., 1996). It is important to consider that, although the organisms that were considered fungi
would correspond to a polyphyletic group, because they do not share a common evolutionary history, equally
3-165
==================================================================================
PAGE 176
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
form are considered closely linked since in many cases their morphology, mode of nutrition and
ecology is convergent (Alexopoulos et al., 1996). The latter motivates, only for practical purposes, that even
continue in many situations, studying each other as a single large group.
The so-called true mushrooms and which are currently classified within the Fu Kingdom
ngi, se
can be defined as eukaryotic organisms, lacking chlorophyll, heterotrophic, with bodies
filamentous, apically elongated, non-motile, although some species have cells
mobile reproductive cells, surrounded by a chitinous cell wall and with a life cycle with reproduction
both sexual and asexual through spores (Alexopoulos et al., 1996; Blackwell & Spatafora, 2004;
Margulis & Schwartz, 1998).
Regarding their diversity, fungi are considered one of the most important groups on the
surface of the earth, where initial estimates suggested the existence of 720,000 to 1.5 million
species (Hawksworth, 1991 and 2001; Mueller & Schmit, 2007; Schmit & Mueller, 2007). On the other hand,
New studies estimate that there would be between 2.2 to 3.8 million species (Hawksworth & Lücking, 2017).
In any case, we have very limited knowledge of fungal diversity (Mueller & Bills,
2004), since, considering these most recent studies, only about 120,000 species have been described, it is
That is, between 3.2 and 5.5% of the fungal diversity worldwide, with small numbers of
representativeness at the national level or with low knowledge of these (Lazo, 1995; Mueller et al., 2007). If
It should be noted that fungi can be found in all environments with adequate conditions of
humidity and temperature, along with available organic substrates. However, the conditions of
Optimal development and reproduction vary widely depending on the species (Alexopoulos et al.,
1996, Margulis & Schwartz, 1998).
A notable characteristic of some fungi is their ability to produce macrostructures
reproductive, which has traditionally caused them to be known as macromycetes,
macrofungi or higher fungi. This grouping is artificial, because both macro and
Micromycetes include many different lineages (RISC, 1997) and is used for practical purposes only. Even so and
Considering the above, macromycetes could be defined as those species capable of producing
large and showy macrostructures, visible to the naked eye, as well as the structure itself (RISC,
1997; Lodge et al., 2004). Macromycetes are mainly fungi belonging to the Phylum
Basidiomycota and, to a lesser extent, the Ascomycota, within the kingdom Fungi.
From an ecological point of view, many fungal species are free-living saprobes, which is very
relevant, because they are generally the primary decomposer organisms in their environments.
This characteristic allows them to use a wide variety of materials as substrates, so they also
can negatively affect man by destroying his food and many elements used in his
activities. Other fungi are biotrophic or parasitic, negatively affecting both animals and humans.
plants. Others are mutualistic, establishing symbiotic relationships with plants, animals and prokaryotes.
3-166
==================================================================================
PAGE 177
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Lichens or lichenized fungi and mycorrhizae are two successful examples of the latter (Alexopoulos et al.,
1996).
On the other hand, lichens are ubiquitous organisms, widely distributed in all conditions.
environments where bare soil occurs, from the tropics and beyond the polar circles
of both hemispheres (e.g. as is the case of the transantarctic mountains).
Regarding their way of life, they are highly successful mutualistic ectosymbionts among members of the Fungi Kingdom.
and different groups of algae, including cyanobacteria, although recent work indicates the presence
more or less stable of a third constitutive fungal component (Spribille et al., 2016). In
In the case of the fungal component, these are obligate or constitutive symbionts, since they need, to
reach maturity and reproduce sexually or vegetatively, being associated with its component
algae In the case of the latter, they are considered facultative symbionts, since they can
appear free-living and complete their biological cycles without the obligatory need to associate with fungi
lichenized (Beck et al., 1998). The third component (secondary fungus) also occurs as
or
obligate symbiont, although its transversality in all lichen groups has yet to be proven
(Spribille et al., 2016). However, the above is the primary fungal component, the most
important (or of greater preponderance) in the association, which can be associated with different algae
depending on the environmental conditions (Vargas & Beck, 2012) and is the organism to which it is assigned
the identity of the thallus or holobiont (Henssen & Jahns, 1973).
Lichenization, in turn, can be considered as the form occupied by fungi, which are organisms
heterotrophs, for the acquisition of new properties and forms of nutrition (Barreno, 1998). Furthermore,
lichens are capable of colonizing a wide range of substrates (Brodo, 1973), not only surfaces
natural resources provided by rocks, soil, bark, decorticated wood, leaves and animal shells, but
also industrial materials (Brightman & Seaward, 1977), such as plastic, rubber, polyethylene,
metals and glass. These characteristics have allowed them to be a constituent part of almost all environments.
where life is possible (Sancho et al., 2006). Although it should be noted that lichens, lacking
conduction or excretion systems, are dependent on the characteristics of their substrates to
settle down. In this way, there is a marked selection for the substrates used by different
species, which, in the long term, can become a specific floristic element associated with a
vegetation formation or a specific environment (Ellis, 2012).
According to the general background presented, taking into consideration the very high diversity that exists
in organisms traditionally known as fungi and the nature of line studies
base, it is established that the fungal groups included in the present study correspond to
macromycetes and lichens, understanding that, although the latter is the term used, it refers
to macrolichens, mainly because their size makes them conspicuous and they can be detected
more easily on the substrates present in the areas to be studied.

ii. General background of the microbiota associated with the area of influence
Regarding the presence of both macromycetes and lichens in the Project Area of Influence,
First of all, it is important to point out that in general the mycological studies of both groups in Chile
They are scarce and shallow. Although it is essential to specify that the scarcity of information
mycology in Chile is not related to a low potential of our mycobiota, rather it is due to the fact that,
Historically, mycological research in Chile has been developed based on specific studies,
carried out mainly by foreign researchers and not a systematic and directed effort by
some national institution or state agency (Sandoval-Leiva, 2014).
In the case of macromycetes, this situation is very marked, since for the central zone of the country there are no
finished catalogs of the present diversity and although there are numerous records of organisms
fungal infections, they are mainly specific records or those that do not correspond to what is commonly
called macromycetes or macrofungi. This can be corroborated by analyzing the work carried out.
by Mujica et al. (1980) and Minter & Peredo (2006), which correspond to the main compilations of
Chilean mushrooms and that contain practically all the fungal records of the country until 1979. In
These works are where, specifically for the Biobío region, you can find
approximately 400 fungal records, of which only about 30 correspond to fungi
macromycetes, while the rest correspond to microscopic fungi, mainly species
phytopathogens that affect agricultural crops in the region.
Anyway, it is possible to list some later works that include records for the region,
mainly what was done by Garrido (1981, 1982, 1983, 1984, 1985 and 1988) and Rajchenberg et al. (2019
and 2021). Furthermore, within the review carried out it is important to mention some
the jobs with records
for the region or relevant from the environmental point of view such as those carried out by Singer (1969)
and Lazo (2001 and 2016).
In the case of lichens, the study of their diversity in Chile has been based mainly on the realization
of floristic lists of different areas of interest in the extreme north and south of the country, or in areas of
particular botanical interest (Galloway & Marticorena, 1991; Galloway, 1998; Quilhot et al., 1998). between
These highlight areas of the Altiplano of the regions of Arica and Parinacota and Tarapacá (Galloway, 1998); oasis
of fog, in the coastal desert, and the coastal zone of central northern Chile (Follmann, 1965, 1967, 1968
and 1995; Follmann & Redón, 1972; Vargas et al., 2017); the temperate rainforest or Valdivian forest (Galloway,
1995); precordilleran areas of central and south-central Chile (Pereira & San Martín, 1998; Pereira et al.,
1999, 2002, 2006 and 2014; Redón & Walkowiak, 1978), from the subantarctic zone of continental and insular Chile
(Øvstedal & Lewis Smith, 2001; Quilhot et al., 2012, Vargas & Morano, 2014; Etayo et al., 2021); and the
Juan Fernández Archipelago (Zahlbruckner, 1924; Redón & Quilhot, 1977).
For the country, the presence of approximately 1,400 species of lichenized fungi has been mentioned.
(Galloway & Quilhot, 1998). This approach is based on bibliographic reviews and could

represent only a low degree of knowledge of the group in the country at the date of publication of the list.
For his part, Follmann (1995) suggests, based on a review of the literature and his experience in the field,
an approximate of 2,500 species for the entire national territory. Recent screenings framed in
The new systematic catalog of Chilean lichens (Vargas & Sandoval-Leiva, 2020) indicates that the
Species richness in Chile would be around 2,500 to 3,000 species.
On the other hand, and in general, ecological studies on population structure and
community in lichenized fungi, with some exceptions in species from the Valdivian forest (Lücking
et al. 2003, Rundel, 1980; Redón, 1973), in their use as model organisms in ecological studies
(Armesto & Contreras, 1981), and in studies of the selectivity of mycobionts for their photobionts in areas
of the coastal desert of the Tarapacá Region (Vargas & Beck, 2012) and in the Magallanes region (Zuñiga
et al., 2015).
For the central-southern area of Chile, the information is relatively extensive, although there are no catalogs.
finishes of the diversity present. Quilhot (1995) estimated the wealth of the VIII region (currently
corresponding to the regions of Ñuble and Biobío) in 69 species, distributed in 26 genera. This
Information was collected from bibliographic reviews, where the majority of the works studied were
of an exploratory nature and associated with the beginning of the naturalistic study in Chile (Galloway & Marticorena
1991; Quilhot, 1995).
On the other hand, the recent systematic catalog of lichens of Chile, which is still in preparation, but
whose first version is online, indicates the presence of approximately 122 species of
lichens and lichenicolous fungi in the Biobío Region (Vargas & Sandoval-Leiva, 2020), this being the
closest estimate of the known diversity for the Region.
3.11.5.2 Taxonomic richness of species in the AI
i. Fungi (macromycetes)
92 taxa of fungi were recorded in the area of influence of the Project during the two survey campaigns.
terrain, which were identified at a specific level in 66.3% of the cases (61 taxa), while
the remaining 33.7% (31 taxa) were identified at the genus level. Systematically, the taxa are
They are organized within the kingdom Fungi, into 2 phyla, 8 classes, 17 orders, 53 families and 81 genera. Of these
Lastly, the genera Crepidotus and Entoloma were the best represented, with 3 taxa each.
In the case of families, the best represented corresponded to Strophariaceae, with 7 taxa, followed by

ii. Lichens
52 taxa of lichens were recorded in the area of influence of the Project during both campaigns, the
that were identified at a specific level in 73.1% of the cases (38 taxa), while 26.9%
remaining (14 taxa) was identified to the genus level. Systematically, the taxa are organized
within the kingdom Fungi, in 1 phylum, 3 classes, 12 orders, 22 families and 33 genera, in addition 1 was recorded
taxon Incertae sedis (of uncertain position) at the family level, according to the sources used. Regarding
To the genera, Chrysothrix, Cladonia, Lecanora, Leptogium and Parmotrema were the best represented,
with 3 species each.
The family with the best representation was Parmeliaceae with 10 taxa. It is followed by the Lecanoraceae family
with 4 taxa. A systematic summary of lichen taxa can be found in Table 3-71.
registered in the AI, while in Table 3-72, the taxonomic catalog can be found in detail
of the taxa.

3.11.5.3 Spatial distribution of the species registered in the AI
After the information gathering campaign, it is possible to point out that in the case of fungi
(macromycetes), the 92 registered taxa were distributed among all the 31 points of
sampling prospects in the area of influence. In the case of lichens, the 52 registered taxa are
distributed among 30 of the 31 prospected sampling points.
The details of the distribution of records of macromycetes and lichens, in the AI, are presented in the
Exhibit 3B-29 and Exhibit 3B-30, respectively. In the indicated Annexes, the occurrences are presented by
sampling point with discovery, in presence/absence matrix format.
3.11.5.4 Environmental distribution of the species registered in the AI
i. Fungi (macromycetes)
The detail of the environmental distribution of macromycetes records in the AI, according to the uses
of soil indicated in Table 3-68, is found in Annex 3B-29, while the summary of the
Results of the analysis of its environmental distribution are presented in Table 3-73.

3.11.5.5 Absolute frequency and relative frequency of recorded taxa
As pointed out in the method
ogy, to obtain an approximate value of the relative importance of
each of the registered taxa, the parameters of absolute frequency and frequency were used
relative.
Yo. Fungi (macromycetes)
According to the methodology used, the recorded fungal taxon that showed the highest frequency in
the AI corresponded to Stereum hirsutum (Figure 3-79) where from its absolute frequency (Fi), it can be
note that it was present in 58.1% of the surveyed sampling points (18 of the 31 points
sampling). Meanwhile, according to its relative frequency (Fri), this species represented 7.1%
of the fungal records made in this study. The detail of the occurrences, the frequencies
absolute frequencies (Fi) and relative frequencies (Fri), of all recorded fungal taxa, is found in
Table 3-75.

3.11.5.6 Conservation Categories of registered species
i. Fungi (macromycetes)
In the case of fungi, 4 species were registered in the AI that present some category proposal
of conservation, in accordance with any of the official species classification processes according to RCE.
In Table 3-77, these species can be reviewed, along with their classification proposal and the process of
classification in which it was proposed.
Table 3-77. Mushroom species in conservation category present in the AI ​​according to the RCE.
Species Conservation Category RCE
Anthracophyllum discolor Least Concern (LC) 11th Process
Cortinarius magellanicus Least Concern (LC) 11th Process
Descolea antarctica Least Concern (LC) 11th Process
Pluteus flammipes Least Concern (LC) 19th Process
Source: GAC

All of these species have been categorized as Least Concern (LC), since they do not
meet the thresholds of any of the criteria to be classified in any of the categories of
IUCN 3.1 threat (Extinct, Extinct in the Wild, Critically Endangered, Endangered or Vulnerable), nor
nor as Near Threatened and its wide distribution indicates that it is not close to satisfying the
criteria. The following can be noted about these species:
Anthracophyllum discolor corresponds to a species of agaricoid fungus, a wood degrader,
abundant and widely distributed in forest environments that range mainly from IV to X
Region.
Cortinarius magellanicus, this species corresponds to an ectomycorrhizal fungus, associated with species of
Nothofagus, with numerous records between Region VII and XII.
Descolea antarctica, this species corresponds to an ectomycorrhizal fungus, associated with species of
Nothofagus, with numerous records between the Metropolitan Region and the XII Region.
Pluteus flammipes, this species corresponds to a fungus associated mainly on wood and bark
decomposing Nothofagus spp. It has records from Bullileo to Llanquihue.

ii. Lichens
Regarding lichens, 8 species were registered in the AI that present some category proposal
of conservation, in accordance with any of the official species classification processes according to RCE.
In Table 3-78, these species can be reviewed, along with their classification proposal and the process of
classification in which it was proposed.
Table 3-78. Lichen species in conservation category present in the AI according to the RCE
Species Conservation Category RCE
Chrysothrix Pavonii Least Concern (LC) 14th Process
Megalaria grossa Least Concern (LC) 16th Process
Parmotrema reticulatum Least Concern (LC) 16th Process
Physcia adscendens Least Concern (LC) 17th Process
Pseudocyphellaria hirsuta Data Deficient (DD) 15th Process
Punctelia subrudecta Least Concern (LC) 17th Process
Ramalina striatula Least Concern (LC) 14th Process
Teloschistes chrysophthalm
us Least Concern (LC) 14th Process
Source: GAC
Of these species, 7 have been categorized as Least Concern (LC). About these species you can
point out the following:
Megalaria grossa is a bipolar species known from temperate environments around the world. In Chile there are
known records in the Maule, Biobío, Aysén and Magallanes region.
Chrysothrix Pavonii is a species that is distributed strictly in South America, with records
known for Argentina, Chile and Peru. In the country, its presence is indicated between the Region of Arica and
Parinacota to the Aysén Region.
Parmotrema reticulatum is a cosmopolitan species, in Chile it is known from Paposo, in the region of
Antofagasta, to Magallanes, including the Juan Fernández archipelago and Rapa-Nui.
Physcia adscendens is a species with a wide bipolar distribution (Elvebakk & Moberg, 2002; Quilhot
et al., 2012), widely distributed in all continental masses of the world, with the exception of the
Antarctica. In Chile it extends from the Valparaíso region to Magallanes, although its
presence in coastal and fog-influenced areas of northern Chile.
Punctelia subrudecta is a cosmopolitan species, with a preference for temperate and Mediterranean climates.
In Chile it is known from the Coquimbo Region to the Magallanes Region. Quilhot et al. (1998) indicate
the presence of the species only in the vicinity of Putre, in the current region of Arica and Parinacota, but not
There are known published records or herbarium materials that confirm this observation.
3-192
==================================================================================
PAGE 203
==================================================================================
Chapter 3 Baseline (Section B)
Terrestrial ecosystems
Mining Development Project for Clay Extraction for Concentrate Production
Rare Earths
Ramalina striatula, is a species widely distributed in tropical to temperate environments of the
southern hemisphere, eminently on the American continent, with presence in the southern part of North
America, Central America, Southeast Asia, Australasia and East Africa. In Chile the species is
widely distributed throughout the country, with records from the Coquimbo Region to the
Magallanes, being the most common species of the genus in the country.
Figure 3-81. Megalaria grossa thallus
Source: GAC
Teloschistes chrysophthalmus is a cosmopolitan species, which in Chile is known from IV to
XII regions (Almborn, 1992), in different environmental conditions, but predominantly in areas
Mediterranean and temperate, occupying multiple substrates, such as plants, rocks or even soil
compacted.
In addition, 1 species categorized as Data Deficient (DD) was recorded, of this species it can be
point out the following:
Pseudocyphellaria hirsuta is a neoaustral species, known only from the southern cone of South America, and in Chile
It is known among the regions from Valparaíso to Magallanes, including the Juan Fernández Archipelago.
Considering that the genus is under review, the species has been classified as Data Deficient
(DD) given that there is high uncertainty about the taxonomic validity of this species.

3.11.5.7 Susceptibility to the effects of Climate Change
In accordance with the criteria established in Resolution No. 202399101883/2023 dated 10
November 202311, of the Environmental Evaluation Service, the Project site area is
would be affected by a reduction in accumulated annual precipitation. According to the percentile
P25 of the available climate projections, this reduction would reach a value of 20.1%, while
According to the P75th percentile of the projections, there would be a decrease of 9.5%. Regarding
trends in temperatures, an increase in the average annual temperature is projected in a
magnitude of 1.4 °C throughout the continental national territory.
Regarding the possible effects of climate change on the fungal and lichen component, this is not
considered among the protection objects potentially vulnerable to
effects of change
climate, in accordance with the guidelines of Table 3 of the “Methodological guide for the consideration
of climate change in the SEIA” (SEA, 2023). The analysis of possible effects of climate change is ruled out
about this component.
3.11.6 Synthesis and conclusions
Through the information gathering campaign in the area of influence of the Project,
92 fungal taxa were recorded, which were distributed in all 31 sampling points.
prospected. Regarding lichens, 52 taxa were recorded, distributed among 30 of the 31 points
sampling.
In relation to the frequency of the species recorded in the AI, in the case of fungi, the most
frequent corresponded to Stereum hirsutum, which was recorded in 58.1% of the sampling points
surveyed (18 of the 31 sampling points), while, in the case of lichens, the most
frequent corresponded to Parmotrma sp, present in 38.7% of the sample points surveyed
(12 of the 31 sampling points).
Regarding the species in conservation category, according to the species classification processes of
According to the RCE, in the case of fungi, 4 of the registered species are categorized
such as Least Concern (LC), Anthracophyllum discolor, Cortinarius magellanicus, Descolea antarctica
and Pluteus flammipes.
In the case of lichens, 8 of the registered species are found under some category of
conservation: 7 of these species are categorized as Least Concern (LC),
Chrysothrix Pavonii, Megalaria grossa, Parmotrema reticulatum, Physcia adscendens, Punctelia
subrudecta, Ramalina striatula and Teloschistes chrysophthalmus. In addition, the species was recorded
Pseudocyphellaria hirsuta, categorized as Data Deficient (DD).
11 Evaluation Criterion in the SEIA: Climate change in the environmental evaluation of water resources.
